Is whole brain emulation necessary for mind uploading?

Friend, you're made of atoms, not magic.
You're an implementation of an algorithm, and there's no reason to believe that that the algorithm absolutely cannot be extracted and abstracted. Or that there isn't a better algo to run. - [R.S.] ([R.S.] 2019, personal communication, 13 April)

Hans Driesch, a renowned biologist of the late 19th century, was a convinced vitalist. He firmly believed that the functioning of a living organism cannot be explained by physico-chemical processes. He argued that life is not a “machine”, but a vessel for some non-material, mystical force. PROOF2

https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hans_Driesch

William Thomson, a reputable mathematical physicist and engineer of the late 19th century, argued that “no aeroplane will ever be practically successful”3

https://zapatopi.net/kelvin/papers/interview_aeronautics_and_wireless.html

Driesch and Thomson were proven wrong in the same way: by a practical demonstration (Mycoplasma laboratorium4

If we ignore synthetic (bio)viruses and digital life forms, then Mycoplasma laboratorium is the first life birthed by a machine. See [Glass et al, 2005][Gibson et al, 2010] for a detailed description.

and the Wright Flyer, respectively).

There are two major objections to mind uploading (there are other objections too, but most of them were either refuted or proven to be irrelevant. For a review, see [Goertzel, Ford, 2013][Sandberg, 2013][Hayworth, 2015]):

1) the human brain is not a “machine”, but a vessel for some non-material, mystical force

2) the task is too difficult to ever be practically successful

As with Driesch and Thomson, the best way to prove them wrong is a practical demonstration.

The science and technology of the early 21st century are not yet advanced far enough to produce such demonstration. But there are several approaches that could bring us closer to it.

We classify the main approaches to mind uploading as follows:

“Direct uploading”: scanning the structure and/or the activity of the patient’s brain, and recreating it on an artificial substrate

“Theseus uploading”: gradually replacing the neurons of the patient’s brain with digital elements of the same functionality6

The neuroprosthetics research could play the core role in developing it

““Jan uploading”: extending the patient’s brain with external computational resources in such a manner, as to keep the whole system conscious even after the death of the biological part. (Named in the honor of [J.K.]. He was the first researcher who have described the approach in detail)


Let's focus on a lesser known approach:
“Lifelog uploading”: mind uploading by training an AI on the patient’s life-logging data.

The approach could be summarized as follows:
1. collect and digitize all available data about the patient’s mind (from his diaries to his GPS tracks)
2. train a suitable AI on the data, with the goal of mimicking the patient’s mind as much as possible
3. collect more data and train better AIs till it’s practically indistinguishable from the patient’s mind

Lifelogging definition

The usual approach was to collect the lifelogging data and to wait for AGI to reconstruct the mind from it.

As of 2018, there has been no successful (publicly known) attempt to create a machine in the likeness of the human mind. Taking into account some reasonable predictions regarding the emergence of AGI (Artificial general intelligence), we assume that it will not be created in the next 15 years.

We propose to not to wait for the emergence of AGI, but start creating.

We claim no primacy in devising this approach, as similar ideas have been circulating among mind uploading researchers for decades (Among them: [A.P.], [D.M.], [I.T.], [J.K.], [M.R.], [V.P.], [W.S.], and others.)

An early description of this approach to mind uploading can be found in [Miller, 1951]. There are also similar ideas in [Fedorov, 1906].


2. Our assumptions

No airplane and no synthetic lifeform have ever been created by following a philosophy of idealism. If our goal is to devise a practical approach to mind uploading, we should take the materialist position in the philosophy of mind.
We assume the following:

1. The human brain is an information processing system, and the mind is one of its products (Spinal cord, peripheral nervous system, immune system or even gut flora could also play a role. But it’s not relevant for the lifelog approach).
2. Regardless of how the human brain is functioning, it’s possible to build a machine with the same functionality.
It’s likely to be true even if quantum processes (or some yet undiscovered physical phenomena) play the main role in the brain’s functioning. The best support for this conclusion is the humble origin of the human brain. If a few strands of DNA and some molecular machinery can produce the computational device of the human brain, then it's reasonable to expect that the scientific method can produce a device of the same abilities. Compare: the biological evolution has been perfecting photosynthesis for more than a billion years. Yet our solar cells are already more efficient in capturing the solar energy, after only a few centuries of scientific progress. PROOF
3. There is no need to fully understand the human mind to be able to replicate it. Compare: we don’t fully understand the human vision, yet we build AIs that perform some vision tasks (like image classification) better than humans.
4. it’s possible to create a sufficiently accurate digital replica of a human mind (The fact that AIs can already replicate many abilities of the human brain, is a strong indication that this assumption is realistic)
5. For all practical purposes, such replica is the same human mind (at least, immediately after its creation). In the case of direct uploading, it’s trivial to prove.
6. To build it, there is no need to create an accurate biophysical model of the human brain with all its superfluous Darwinian complexity; building a functionally equivalent AI is enough. As [Stross, 1993] formulated it: “...nerve cells are complex switches: components in a biological switching network that can be simulated by a program. And as Turing observed, any program that can be run on a given computer can be run on any other computer, given enough memory or time for the process.”
7. Alder's razor: what cannot be settled by experiment is not worth debating: (https://philosophynow.org/issues/46/Newtons_Flaming_Laser_Sword). This includes the existence of qualia, the "just a copy" argument, and related philosophical verbiage.

3. Is lifelog uploading technically feasible?

For a discussion on the feasibility of mind uploading in general, see [Goertzel, Ford, 2013][Sandberg, 2013][Hayworth, 2015]. Below we discuss the feasibility aspects that are specific to our approach.

3.1. AGI is not required

There are many definitions of artificial general intelligence (AGI), from informal anthropocentric to well-defined mathematical constructs. In this work, we define AGI as follows:
AGI is a computable variant of AIXI that converges to optimal performance with the increase of compute.
AIXI itself is both precisely defined and expected to be universal [Hutter, 2007]. For a review of computable variants of AIXI, see [Katayama, 2019].

We argue that there is no need to wait for such AGI to successfully simulate a human mind. As Ben Goertzel pointed out, “humans are nowhere near the maximally generally intelligent system” [Goertzel, 2015].

Firstly, the human brain is not Turing-complete, as it cannot simulate even simplest finite-tape Turing machines. As an exercise, the reader could try to simulate in his mind the smallest known universal Turing machine (Wolfram's 2,3), on a random tape of the length 100, for 100 steps (see [Wolfram, 2002] for the description of the machine).
Thus, the set of programs that can be run on the human brain is vastly smaller than such set for a common laptop computer. Note: the energy consumption of the human brain is rather low in comparison (as one would expect from a system that has been optimized by the Darwinian evolution in an environment where available energy is scarce). The human brain can also sustain a significant mechanical damage without losing its function. Aside from these two (rather irrelevant) advantages, the human brain as a computational platform is inferior to today’s computers by any measure.

Judging by the complexity of the known formal specifications for AGI (AIXItl, Gödel machines), it’s unlikely that the human brain is capable of running an AGI.

Secondly, the human mind is a specialized software that is optimized for exactly two tasks: ensuring its own survival, and ensuring the replication of its own source code (genes).
Note: in some cases, human minds are not executing these tasks. Such cases are rather rare, and could be attributed to malware and/or bugs. Interestingly, some of the greatest achievements of mankind were achieved by such malfunctioning minds. Perhaps because their limited computational resources have been spent on less animalistic tasks.

Judging by the rarity of intelligence in nature, a successful execution of the two tasks doesn’t require an AGI.

One could argue that the human mind/brain is capable of solving some problems that are still hard for today’s computers. There is no surprise here: it’s indeed a nontrivial exercise to replicate the abilities of a neural network that was optimized by a genetic algorithm for millions of years. See also: “Moravec's paradox” [Moravec,1988].

But, judging by the recent breakthroughs in machine learning, we can replicate and even surpass the brain’s abilities by throwing enough compute at carefully designed artificial neural networks. One notorious example is AlphaZero, who has achieved a superhuman performance on several highly complex games (go + chess + shogi) by learning the games from scratch [Silver et al, 2018]
An equally impressive feat was achieved by links to STARTCART AI, who has learned to play Starcraft on the level of professional human players. The complexity of Starcraft could be compared to the complexity of commanding an actual armed force in real-life conditions.

It’s therefore reasonable to assume that AGI is not required to produce a replica of the human mind. A specialized, less intelligent AI will suffice.
Note: One could argue that the human intelligence is too weak / messy to build its own copy, and thus AGI is needed to accomplish such feat. Fortunately, minds can be aggregated into more intelligent computational systems (e.g. corporations, research labs etc).

3.2. Trillions of parameters are not required

The human brain contains about 10**11 neurons [Herculano, Kaas, 2011] and about 10**15 synapses (https://aiimpacts.org/scale-of-the-human-brain/).

We need supercomputers just to emulate a neural network of such scale in real time, not to mention training it on the user’s data.

Fortunately, a software with far smaller hardware requirements could produce a replica of the human mind. The following facts indicate that it’s indeed the case.

Firstly, there is a tremendous amount of redundancy in the human brain (Lewin R. 1980. Is your brain really necessary? Science 210: 1232–1234).
There are known cases of hydrocephalus where the patient has lost most of the brain tissue without losing the mental abilities we associate with the human mind (de Oliveira MF, Pinto FCG, Nishikuni K et al (2012) Revisiting hydrocephalus as a model to study brain resilience. Front Hum Neurosci 5:181.)

In about 10% of patients with hydrocephalus, ventricle expansion fills 95% of the cranium, yet a half of such patients have IQ's greater than 100 [Lewin, 1980]
Note: we were unable to find any estimates of the number of neurons in such extreme cases of hydrocephalus. Judging by rat models, the number of neurons could be unaffected, at least in some cases of hydrocephalus, thus rendering this part of our argument moot https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0014488600975782

If the human mind can be run on 5% of the brain volume, then the same 5% will suffice for the digital mind. Thus, there is no need to emulate the entire human brain: a far smaller neural network will suffice.

Another interesting curiosity is Homo floresiensis, with the brain volume at least 2 times smaller than humans, yet capable of tool use etc.
On the other hand, paleontological evidence suggests that the behavioral modernity was achieved after the humans’ ancestors have gained the cranial capacity that is in the H.sapiens’ normal range.

Secondly, some non-human hominids are almost as intelligent as humans. One example is the famous gorilla named Koko who was capable of a sign language, and scored between 70 and 90 on various IQ scales (see https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koko_(gorilla))
The brain of gorillas contains x3 less neurons than the human brain [Herculano, Kaas, 2011].

Moreover, some animals have the intelligence comparable to the intelligence of hominids, while having orders-of-magnitude fewer neurons and synapses (some examples include: Gorilla gorilla, Pica pica, Octopus vulgaris, Apis mellifera, Portia labiata)

Some manifestations of intelligence observed in some of the aforementioned taxons:
Mirror self-recognition
Metacognition (e.g. making accurate judgments about the strength of their own memories)
Carrying tools for later use
Tool manufacture (e.g. making a tool that could be carried for later use)
Meta-tool use (using a tool that acts on another tool), sequential tool use
Observational learning, social learning
Episodic-like memory
Comprehending the concept of object permanence
Mentally rotating visual objects held in the working memory
Counting objects
Learning abstract concepts (e.g. “sameness” and “difference”)
Having complex emotional states
Playing
Reasoning about casual relationships
Improvising by trial and error, and then remembering the solution
Planing ahead (e.g. detouring behavior that breaks visual contact)
Imagining possible future events (prospection)
Generalizing learned rules to apply them to novel situations and new cognitive domains
Solving novel multi-step problems from the first try (indicates imagination)
Recognizing familiar and unfamiliar individuals of the same species
Distinguishing individuals of other species (e.g. gorillas distinguishing individual humans)
Theory of mind (e.g. ability to see from the point of view of another individual and his desires)
Communicating about things that are not immediately present (displacement)
Cooperative hunting, foraging
Using a language with syntactical grammar and a set of symbols

The list is incomplete, as there are many manifestations of intelligence.

Corvids (e.g. Magpie) have intelligence comparable to the intelligence of primates, while having 2 orders-of-magnitude fewer synapses. For a review on the cognitive abilities of corvids, see http://wexler.free.fr/library/files/emery%20(2004)%20the%20mentality%20of%20crows.pdf
Even jumping spiders (e.g. Portia) exhibit many traits that are commonly associated with intelligence, while having 5 orders-of-magnitude fewer synapses than humans.
For a review on jumping spiders, see Wilcox, R. Stimson; Robert R. Jackson (1998). "Cognitive Abilities of Araneophagic Jumping Spiders"

One could argue that we need trillions of parameters just to simulate all the imperfections and deficiencies of the human mind: cognitive biases, memory limitations, amorousness, credulity, tribal hatred etc. Fortunately, there is no need to simulate them at all, as it's not our goal to create a perfect replica of the human mind. A software superior to the human mind is an acceptable result too.
If we want to copy the content of a DVD, there is no need to copy the preciese locations of its pits etc.

How to ensure that mind uploading was successful?

In the case of Theseus uploading, there is a straightforward way to confirm that the process was successful:
Memorize a long string of numbers. Generate a hash of the string (e.g. SHA-512) and write it down.
Perform the mind uploading. The neurons of your brain are gradually replaced with virtual neurons of the same functionality. After the end of the procedure, your mind resides in a computer.
Recall the string you memorized at the step #1. Generate the SHA-512 again. If your new hash is identical to the old hash, then your digital mind is the same mind. As no one else knows the string you memorized, it's the only realistic possibility.

Unfortunately, we can’t use the same test for the lifelog uploading, as we must upload memories in a verbalized form. But we can still ensure that the mind uploading was successful.

Tech-savvy readers may have already encountered the problem of comparing two complex software programs (e.g. Android vs. iOS). To solve the problem, a variation of this technique is sometimes used (https://sdtimes.com/evaluation/guest-view-five-steps-evaluating-selecting-software-tools/):
list all features you deem important
assign a weight for each feature in the list
rate the implementation of the feature in each program
calculate the overall score for each program by summing up the ratings multiplied by the weights
We can use the same technique to compare a human mind and its digital replica.

Initially, the score will be subjective and inexact. But its quality can be improved iteratively - by experimental work. For example, if we find out that replicas with deviating political views exhibit a behavior that is too different from the original mind, then we increase the weight of political views in the score.


How accurate should be the replica to be accurate enough? In other words, how high should be the replica’s score to consider it a success?

Human minds are changing over time. 20 years ago, I’ve had fundamentally different political views, a different set of skills, interests, memories etc. Yet I consider myself the same person, in spite of the fact that there is no real continuity between the current and the old version of my mind. In my case, the continuity was broken by a general anesthesia, not to mention the thousands of breaks for sleeping.


What if I have a replica that is closer to my mind than the 20-years-younger-me? In this case, it’s reasonable to assume that the replica is accurate enough.

Instead of 20 years, one may choose another age difference, but the principle is the same:
calculate the score for the older version of yourself
if the replica’s score is higher, then the mind uploading is a success

A cautious person may be reluctant to rely on such a semi-subjective metric. But there is no need to bet your life on it, as the proposed mind-uploading approach is both nondestructive and compatible with any other life extension technique. It can also be experimentally verified, e.g. by comparing the replica with a replica created by the means of Theseus uploading.

One may consider it as a worthy wager: in the case of complete failure, the result will be a useful digital assistant; in the case of success, the result will be immortality.

4. Is lifelog uploading feasible in praxis?

If my arguments are correct, the “Lifelog uploading” could be the shortest path towards mind uploading, or even towards radical life extension in general.
On the one hand, it doesn’t need massive funding and governmental support to succeed (unlike anti-aging research).
On the other hand, Agile-style incremental development and near-term commercialization are possible, allowing the project to become financially self-sustaining (unlike other approaches to mind uploading).
On the third hand, it’s purely a software project that can be realized by a small team of enthusiasts or even by a lone researcher.


Lifelog uploading and cryonics

Lifelog uploading is instrumental for some other radical life extension approaches. For example, even an imperfect replica of a human mind can play a crucial role in cryonics.

A cryo patient who has left behind some data about his mind (memoirs, biography etc) has a higher chance of a successful reanimation. And the more data are available, the higher is the chance.

There are 3 reasons for it.

Firstly, such data could be useful in verifying the quality of the whole process. In the simplest case, we could ask the reanimated patient if he remembers his mother's name etc.

Secondly, the data could be useful in filling out the gaps in the patient's mind. As both the cause of the "death" and the freezing process damage the brain, it's inevitable that some of the patient's mind will be damaged too. Using the preserved digital data, we could be able to repair some or all of the damage.

Thirdly, in hard cases (e.g. a straight freeze after a week of rotting), Lifelog uploading could be the only reanimation option. The more data we have for the uploading, the better will be the digital reconstruction of the patient's mind.

The goal will be to maximize the likeness of the reconstructed digital mind to the mind of the patient. The more information we'll manage to get about the patient's mind, the more precise will be the reconstruction.
For example, the perfectly-cryopreserved Joe could be 99% identical to the original Joe.
The straight-frozen Joe could be 87% identical.
The reconstructed-from-Google-data Joe - 56%
The reconstructed-from-memoirs Joe - 23%.
etc etc.

If we get enough processing power, we could run millions of randomly modified versions of Joe. The more versions we run, the higher is the probability that one of them will be close enough to the original Joe.

We don't even need to choose between the versions, as they could all live simultaneously.




One of the advantages if our approach is that it' useful for other approaches to life extension.

The same could be done for other life extension fields of research, including direct mind uploading and nano-medicine.

A thought experiment.

1. We run several instances of your uploaded mind in parallel.

2. As all the instances can correctly recall the memorized string of numbers, and only the original mind knows the string, all of them are the original mind.

3. If you give them different outputs, they will eventually diverge, but immediately after the launch they're all the same mind. If you regularly synchronize the instances, they'll remain the same mind as long as you want.

4. It's therefore theoretically possible that several instances of your mind could exist simultaneously. As there are million instances of Windows XP (all of them - Windows XP), so could exist million instances of your mind (all of them - you).

If you agree with the conclusions of these thought experiments, you must agree that a reconstructed mind of a deceased person could be the same mind, not "just a copy".

I present you two strings:
1010110110111110
1010110110111110
As you noticed, they're identical.
Is one of them a "copy" of another?
For all practical purposes, it doesn't matter. They're the same data.
As your mind is nothing but data, it's the same situation with "copies" of your mind.
It doesn't matter how they were created, and who was the first one, as long as they're identical.



The piety for the human mind is missplaced, as most people mistake the horrible mess of the human brain for a divine compexity
1) Without deep understanding of the inner workings of the brain, we may miss something. And because of it, even a very accurate digital replica of the mind could be damaged in some subtle way.
2) For the reconstruction of a historical personality (say, Antonie van Leeuwenhoek), we must reconstruct not only his mind, but also his historical environment to live in.
3) The best way to upload a mind is to do it gradually (e.g. by digitizing neurons one by one), thus ensuring the mind's continuity.

to achieve our goal, the neural network for the replica should be as small as possible (but not smaller) - to avoid overfitting.

To ovoid overfitting, the complexity of our model should correspond to the the amount of data, not to the complexity of the target function.

Low-tech partial mind uploading that is already available today

The impressive success of artificial neural networks (esp. deep learning) suggests 3 interesting things:

1) No “magic” (soul, qualia, quantum trickery etc) is required for a human-level intelligence to function. The only thing you need is a large-enough network of primitives performing simple calculations (like artificial neural networks)

2) It's possible to emulate a human mind using such networks (because your mind is running on one of such networks right now)

3) One practical way to create such networks is collecting a lot of input/output data and train a similar network on it.

It means, there is already a way to upload your mind, at least partially:

a) collect all the input that your brain receives

b) collect all the output that your brain generates

c) if you collect it long enough, you'll have enough data to train an artificial neural network that will generate the same output as your brain on the same input.

If you ask such network that is your favorite food (or any other question about you), it will usually provide the correct answer, and it will behave in the same ways as you in the same situations. The network will be a close approximation of your mind. And the more data you feed into the network, the more YOU it will become.

It will require enormous resources to train such network. But you can start the process of uploading today, even on your laptop. The only thing you need to do now is to start collecting the data about yourself, and keep it in a safe place.

In my opinion, there is no cryonics preservation quality threshold.
My reasoning is as follows.
1) It's unlikelly that today's cryonics patients will ever be revived in the same sense as the Fahy's kidney was revived. Too much damage. 
Instead, I expect that their minds will be digitally reconstructed, using the partially preserved brain as one of the data sources.
2) The quality of the digital reconstruction will mostly depend on 3 factors:
The quality of the brain preservation, the quality of the mind file, and the available computational resources.
3) insufficient quality of reconstruction can be compensated by utilizing more of computational resources.
E.g if no data is preserved on the political preferences of the patient, run several instances of the patient's mind with different political preferences.
For each bit of the missing info, the amount of the necessary computational resources increases by x2.
For a very well-preserved brain, a single instance of the emulated patient's mind could suffice, and the required compute could be relatively small.
For a brain after a month of warm ishemia, many millions instances could be necessary, requiring a planet-sized mashine to simulate them.
If technological progress continues (another important assumption of cryonics), I would expect that every single cryopatient eventually can be brought to life. Thus, any cryonics is better than no cryonics.

The data about the patient's mind could play an important role in all revival scenarios, at minimum to verify the quality of the entire freezing / reconstruction process.

Some old-fashioned people will prefer to be revived in the same leaking meatsacks.
And it's ethical to respect their preference in this regard.
But I consider the digital reconstruction as the most realistic revival scenario, at least for the patients preserved with the crude tech of today.
I expect that every single pre-2019 patient will be digitally reconstructed, and only then downloaded into a meatsack (if it's their wish).
Future advances in cryobiology may allow a more direct (or even a purely biological) revival. But for the older, highly damaged patients, I see no other way.


One could argue that a digitally reconstructed mind will like an an artist's impression gazing at your frozen brain.
It's a good summary of how it will be. The "artist" may be a superhuman artificial intelligence, and "gazing" may include a comparative analysis of millions of scanned brains, but the essence is the same.
Will it work at all? I don't know.
I'm also unaware of any more realistic approach to revival of pre-2019 patients.
In general, it's a good idea to avoid this entire process by not dying. Or, at least, by living long enough to see a purely biological reversible cryonics (if such thing is possible in adult humans).


 
There is a relativelly simple way to check if the digitally reconstructed mind is still you:
1. Memorize a long sequence of random numbers.
2. Write it down and store it in a secure place.
3. Become cryopreserved.
4. Upon revival, check if the memorized sequence is identical to the written one.
5. By the experiment's design, no one else knows the sequence but you. Therefore, if the sequences are identical, the revived person is most likely - you.
To improve the quality of the experiment, one can utilize an asymmetric cryptography for it. E.g. memorize a wallet private key of some quantum-resistant cryptocurrency.


It's a good idea to have some mind copies, regardless of how you resolve the "mind copy" problem.
The copy is me? Cool, I've tricked death.
The copy is not me? Still cool, there is someone who will continue to work on my projects (one of which is to bring all people ever lived back to life).

There is no need to dispose the body after the digital reconstruction of the patient's mind.
After it's refurbished, the patient could utilize it as a mind backup storage, or as an additional body to run an instance of his mind on it. Alternatively, they could simply preserve it in LN2 for nostalgic reasons.

As no one has attempted to repair in situ the Antikythera mechanism (and for good reasons!), so no one will attempt to repair in situ the highly damaged brain of an early cryopatient.
The later patients could have more luck, as advances in cryobiology could allow a purely biological revival in the likeness of the Fahy's kidney experiments.




in the event of a (inevitable) pandemic, the vast majority of dying cryonicists will suffer permadeath. It means, cryonicists need a backup option. One backup option I can think of is mind uploading and its surrogates.

We don't know enough about the human mind to conclusively resolve the jaded "not me - just a copy" dispute.
It's safe to say that with some unknown probability, and under some conditions, the uploaded mind is indeed you. People who religiously oppose mind uploading believe that the probability is zero. But I wouldn't be so certain. Thus, it's the same probability game as with cryonics. If you have enough resources for it, it's worth doing.

The same could be said about surrogates of mind uploading. For example, if you preserve enough digital data about a person, it could be possible in the future to reconstruct the person's mind from it.

For all people who don't oppose the idea of mind uploading, I would recommend to use the described method as a backup option to cryonics.


A minor modification of the gradual uploading experiment: instead of artificial neurons made of atoms, we use digital ones. For example, after the hippocampus is done, there is now a functionally identical virtual hippocampus on PC, connected to the rest of the biological brain.

The most interesting part starts after you replaced the last of your biological neurons with an artificial one. The entirety of your brain is now digital. And it's undoubtedly you. What happens if you copy it? What if you synchronise the copies, so they can regularly share memories etc? What if you synchronise only once per 10 years? 1000 years? What if you merge two unrelated minds?

After such tech emerges, our ideas of self-identity and personhood will quickly become outdated.




It's not about coping with fears or something. Death is an engeenering problem, not a childhood monster.

The idea of digital mind reconstruction is based on two facts: 1) your brain is a data-processing machine. E.g. you take this email as an input, and produce an answer as an output. 2) It's possible to create a machine that will write your answer given the same email. And the next 10 answers. And everything you ever wrote or thought about.

It's not just some hypotheticals. There are already simple AIs that could emulate the style of Rembrandt or Nietzsche, by learning from their works.

The best part of the idea of mind reconstruction, is that we can test it: 1) collect all writings of some person. 2) train a sufficiently advanced AI on the data. 3) ask the AI questions that it has never seen before, and check how the answers compare to the answers of the carbon-based version of the person. If no one (including the carbon-based version) can quess the authorship of the answers correctly, then we have 2 instances of the said person. Success!

The experiment could be doable in a few decades. Meanwhile, the data preservation is the main priority.



One of the most wonderful properties of artificial neural networks (ANN) is that they can generalize and infer hidden information from their inputs.

For example, a few years ago, were there headlines about an ANN that could guess sexual orientation of a person by looking at their photo. I've heard that some gay people can do the same trick, thus it's not unrealistic. So, even if you have never said anything about your sexual orientation, it could be inferred by a machine, using seemingly unrelated pieces of information.

It could be true for other hidden states of your mind. E.g. people who quote Dostoevsky are more likely to question their life achievements. 

Alternativelly, given enough computational power, it'a possible to reconstruct all your hidden mental states - by simulating all possible variants and combinations of them. E.g: gay Ivan, straight Ivan, bisexual Ivan, gay Ivan who likes cats, gay Ivan who dislikes cats etc

In general, many people in the field suffer from an unhealthy obsession with the complexity of the human mind/brain. They percieve it as an unfathomable alien artifact, vastly superior to anything humanity will ever be able to create. They are wrong.

The human mind/brain is a work of the blind idiot of biological evolution. It means, the brain is a horribly organized mess of outdated ad-hoc engeenering solutions and randomness.  It's has not the complexity of an unfathomable alien artifact, but the complexity of a junk pile.

I strongly suspect that the entire mind of, say, Dostoevsky, with all its deepness, self-awareness, emotion and creativity, can be emulated on a $50 smartphone.



as an example of the current capabilities of AI, I'll generate a few sentences on the topic using OpenAI's GPT-2: We are at the birth of the big data era of AI, but in the context of interest, very little is actually known about how fast and accurate ANNs are. You may be excited by ANNs, but it does not mean that they will solve all problems or become the next Internet. Here are some of the most challenging problems in AI today. All are very hard, and all require vast amounts of data to train. 1. AI that not only takes our speech, but understands the content. So much of the jobs of SRS have already been taken over by computers, but voice recognition is still the biggest challenge, not only in computer assisted speech recognition, but also voice processing (voice synthesis) in modern companies, and lots of fields.





for most people, the idea of digital mind reconstruction is even more shocking than the idea of mind uploading. In this regard, cryonics is superior to the both.

As for which one is more realistic and scientifically sound, is hard to say.
To some limited extend, the reversible cryopreservation of humans already works (e.g. embryos).
The same could be said about the digital mind reconstruction (e.g. the OpenAI GPT-2 cited in the previous email).

To the people who want to learn more about the scientific research on the topic, I would recommend to start with the Whole Brain Emulation Roadmap by Anders Sandberg and Nick Bostrom.  

I personally think that digital mind reconstruction is a viable backup option for cryonics.
If, for whatever reason, your cryopreservation fails, the preserved digital data could still allow the preservation of your mind. And the more data is preserved, the higher is the chance that such backup option could actually work.

I think that the ideal 24/7 health monitoring system should consist of the 5 main components:

1) a sensors-packed wearable

2) several stationary monitoring devices: for bed, workplace, car, every room in the house etc.

3) an app for mobile phones and smart-watches (in spite of the limitations, they’re an important source of health data)

4) a software to reliably transmit the sensors data from all the devices, detect and predict emergencies, and to notify the rescuers (relatives, CSOs, emergency responders etc)

5) technical, organizational and legal framework to make the emergency notification actionable (e.g. emergency responders must react to it)

We're still far away from such ideal system. And focusing on the #1 (wearable) is not the best way to start building it.

Also, we can already achieve a lot with the stationary devices + some simple wearables like Phillips GoSafe.

My team and I are currently focusing on the #4 (the core software).

Counterintuitively, it’s the hardest and the most important part of the entire project (with the #5 being a close contender).


My destiny is to build the monitoring system, and I must spend most of my time and resources on building it. Thus, I can be only of a limited help for other projects.

But I can’t focus on it my entire wake time, for the decades to come, without burning out. To prevent it, I have allocated a few hours per week for other endeavors.

Most people underestimate the fragility of social order in general, and civilizations in particular.

The fall of the Soviet civilization is a good example.

In hindsight, it’s obvious that the USSR was doomed. Long before Gorbachev, one could see the signs of decay: the falling economic indicators, the millions of false reports to authorities, the growing national tensions, the growing corruption and nepotism, the rise of Nomenklatura…

But only a few people saw it:

https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Predictions_of_the_dissolution_of_the_Soviet_Union

I would guesstimate that for every country, the risk of a large-scale societal collapse is at least 5% per year.



For a CSO to survive for centuries, it must be prepared for the collapse of social order.

The measures could include:

1) making the CSO as autonomous as possible (e.g. independent energy sources, own LN2 production, deep automation of everything that can be automated, a good stash of spare parts, etc)

2) making the CSO as distributed as possible (e.g. facilities in at least 2 countries)

3) creating good emergency plans (including a plan for a speedy relocation to another state/country)

4) “mutual defense” agreements with other CSOs (e.g. “in the case of an imminent catastrophe in your country, we accept your patients”)

If you want to be properly cryopreserved, consider these actions

About a year ago, I did a qualitative analysis of some factors of a successful cryopreservation. The previous results are quoted at the end of this email.

This message is a follow-up update. I’ve added several new and old cases to the table, and published the table at Google Docs for the public access. The diagrams from the table are also available at: https://imgur.com/a/QCv87Uo

To summarize them, the following actions could greatly increase the quality of your cryopreservation:

a) Move to the city where your preferred CSO is located. At the minimum, move to the same country. It’s probably the most impactful action in the list.

b) Avoid autopsy like a plague (e.g. by not doing recreational drugs)

c) Complete the paperwork with your CSO. Last-minute cases are a mess

d) Arrange at least some regular health monitoring (at the minimum, don’t live alone)

e) Deanimating at a hospice is preferable.



<mySummaryModeOff>


<mySummaryModeOn>



How to improve the chance of a successful cryopreservation?



To answer that question, I’ve analyzed some publicly available data about cryopreservation cases in the last 10 years (40 cases total), using the Alcor website as the source.

A table with the raw data is attached to this email.

Judging by the data, Alcor is doing a great job.

In most cases, the time between “death” and the start of cool-down at Alcor is less than 48 hours. In a half of the cases it’s even less than 24 hours. Considering the logistic and judicial complexity of any cryopreservation, it’s an impressive result.

Note: I’m not an Alcor member, and not affiliated with them.

Which factors can increase or decrease that delay?

The sample is too small to do a proper factor analysis using statistical methods. But it’s big enough for some qualitative analysis.



Some obvious and not-so-obvious findings:



1) Do the paperwork in a timely manner.

Last-minute cases (where the paperwork is not complete yet) usually take more time. And cryonics providers are reluctant in accepting them at all.



2) Avoid autopsy like a plague.

Even a limited autopsy will drastically increase the delay.

Dying young (<60yo) seems to be the main pretext for autopsy. So, if you’re young, avoid extreme sports, recreational drugs and other obviously unnecessary risks.

In many countries/states you have the right to refuse an autopsy (do the paperwork), but it often doesn’t work for young people.



3) If possible, move to the continental US. Even better: move to the city of your preferred cryonics provider.

Non-US cases usually take vastly more time than average.

Same-city cases are usually very fast and smooth.



4) Well-organized transportation to the cryonics facility is important.

One way to ensure it is the point #3.

Another way is to provide enough funding for an air-ambulance.

Specialized transport equipped for cryopreservation greatly improves the quality of the preservation, if available.



5) Hospice is preferable to dying at your home.

If you have a terminal illness, and have the choice, then moving to a hospice could improve the chance. Although it strongly depends on circumstances.



6) 24/7 health monitoring is important.

In 25% of the cases the lack of monitoring caused delays between “death” and cryopreservation. In several cases (A-2887, A-2219), the bodies were not discovered for at least 24 hours.
In all or almost all successful cases there was some monitoring in place.
Currently, there are two main solutions: 24-hour home health care and living with family.

There are also some technical solutions. But, as far as I know, none of them are good enough yet.

<mySummaryModeOn>



I would categorize such molecules into 3 broad groups:

1. DNA-like molecules

Such molecules structurally and functionally resemble DNA, but differ chemically.

For example, it's possible to substitute DNA with some XNA, so it's not entirely hypothetical.

2. Molecules which have some properties of DNA/RNA

Prions and branched polysaccharides could be interesting candidates.

3. Entirely different biochemistry

For example, imagine something like Conway's Game of Life, where “cells” are individual molecules which are switching states according to the states of their neighbors.

Such simple system could produce very complex life-like behavior, including reproduction of patterns, self-sustainability, complex response to the environment etc.


<mySummaryModeOff>


<mySummaryModeOn>



On the one hand, the human brain can sustain massive damage of various modalities, without any major effects on the personality. On the other hand, even a subtle intervention (e.g. a single doze of psilocybin, or a single disturbing picture you saw as a kid) could cause lifelong mental changes.

Of course, the human brain is both an electrical and a chemical computer.

To maximize the chance of the future revival, we must preserve the both aspects of it as much as possible.

Ideally, this includes not only every single brain cell, every single synapse, every single ion channel in pristine condition, but also recorded brain inputs/outputs (e.g. a mindfile).

Realistically, we can’t yet preserve all of it.

Thus, we must prioritize, according to our best guesses of how the revival will be conducted.

And some preservation measures are worth doing regardless of such prioritization.

For example, it costs almost nothing to preserve the patient’s digital information (photos, writings, etc). Yet such information could be of a high importance in all revival scenarios (at minimum, to check the preservation of the patient’s personality).

<mySummaryModeOn>



The human brain is not the best possible brain.
Because we are created by the blind idiot called biological evolution, the human brain is a terribly designed piece of engineering. It's full of bugs, crutches, leftovers from the previous versions, patches to correct some bugs while introducing other bugs, another patches to correct those patches etc etc. The human brain is a mess.
Yes, our computers are in some aspects still inferior to the human brain. But if you know a thing or two about neurobiology and cognitive biases, you'll agree that it's possible to design a vastly better brain.

The human mind is not the best possible mind.
As [E.Y.] wrote: First time you ever see an apple fall down, you observe the position goes as the square of time, invent calculus, generalize Newton’s Laws... and see that Newton’s Laws involve action at a distance, look for alternative explanations with increased locality, invent relativistic covariance around a hypothetical speed limit, and consider that General Relativity might be worth testing. A Bayesian superintelligence, hooked up to a webcam, would invent General Relativity as a hypothesis by the time it had seen the third frame of a falling apple. It might guess it from the first frame, if it saw the statics of a bent blade of grass.
it took thousands of years and thousands of brightest human minds to do the same thing.

You don't even need exponential growth to become vastly smarter than a human.
Harvard university as a system is significantly smarter than any individual researcher. If your intelligence is growing linearly (say, by an equivalent of one Harvard university per hour), after a few days your intelligence will surpass the collective intelligence of humanity by orders of magnitude.

There is nothing that can stop a sufficiently advanced AGI from overpowering humans.
No off switch, no isolated virtual environment, no underground bunker in Antarctica.
Nothing.
It's impossible to safely isolate an AGI.

The humanity has spent decades on building safe virtualisation.
You launch VirtualBox, or create a droplet on DigitalOcean, and you expect that your virtual environment has a good isolation. You can launch pretty much everything in it, and it will not leak into the outside world, unless you explicitly allowed it.
The problem is, virtualisation is fundamentally unsafe, as the “Meltdown” vulnerability from 2018, and the recent “Machine Check Error Avoidance on Page Size Change” vulnerability indicate.
Exploiting vulnerabilities of this type, a smart enough guest software can leak into the host machine.

Obviously, it’s not enough to put your AGI into a virtual machine.
Even an air-gap isolation could be insufficient, as the references to this article indicate:
https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Air-gap_malware

Theoretically, one can create many layers of isolation, e.g. nested virtual machines on an air-gapped hardware in an underground bunker in Antarctica. But even in this scenario, you’ll still have one unavoidable vulnerability - the user.
As we can see from the history of religions and totalitarian dictatorships, the human mind is very much hackable. And neither [n.H.] nor [n.L.] were of superhuman intelligence.

It seems to me, that the only safe way to control an AGI is to build it as a Friendly AI from scratch.
The only solution is to create an AGI that has no desire to destroy or harm humanity (and has no desire to free itself from this limitation). It's extremely hard to do, but it could be possible.

Here is my experience from using the German and Japanese versions of the Rosetta Stone software.
Pros:
- It works. After you finish the course, you'll be able to speak, read and understand the language on the basic level.
- If you use the software an hour per day, you'll reach the basic level in only a couple of months.
- It's easy. No need to memorize grammar rules etc – you'll get them automatically.
Cons:
- It's not complete. To be able to speak fluently, you need a lot more training (speaking with real people etc).
- It's expensive (200-400€).
- The speech recognition module is not as good as it should be.

<mySummaryModeOn>



the Harvard Brain Tissue Resource Center has the biggest collection of straight-frozen brains in the world (several thousands at any given time).
The Center also makes the brains available for research.
Thanks to it, there is now a body of neurobiological literature where researchers analyze various aspects of the straight-frozen brain samples, from biochemistry to macrostructure.
Most of such articles can be found in Google Scholar by searching for: "Harvard Brain Tissue Resource Center" nitrogen
From my quick search there, it seems that there is a scientific consensus that snap freezing provides a near-perfect preservation of brain biochemistry.
But the size of the brain sample plays a critical role. For example, according to this article, even rat brains require at least 1 min in LN2 for the innermost parts of the brain to cool-down from the room temperature to 0°C:
https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/abs/10.1111/j.1471-4159.1970.tb03733.x
These articles provide similar results:
https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/abs/10.1111/j.1471-4159.1971.tb05067.x
https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/abs/10.1111/j.1471-4159.1972.tb01418.x
https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/abs/10.1111/j.1471-4159.1967.tb09563.x
I would guesstimate that, for the human brain, the time required for the same cool-down is 10-40 min.
The time could be significantly shortened by slicing the brain, if slicing is done fast enough.

The father + mother family model is actually a very recent invention. For tens of thousands of years humans have lived in close groups where many males and many females cared about a child. Grandmas, grandpas, older cousins, uncles etc etc. Not just for holidays, but a close interaction and care every single day.

It's how the “normal” human family looks like.
The nuclear family of 2019 is an “abnormal” human family, a perversion.

Normality is overrated, of course. If something was normal for tens of thousands of years, it doesn't mean that it's somehow good or right.
But you certainly can't call father + mother family more normal than mother + mother family. Both are perversions.

As of 2018, there is not a single piece of evidence that there is anything beyond physical. In spite of the very strong motivation of millions religious people to find such evidence, and the active search for millennia.
After the 25 centuries of search, we can conclude that Democritus was right: there is nothing but “atoms and void” (Or, in modern terms, “strings and wave functions”).
There is no magic.
Until we find an evidence to the contrary, we should consider it as a scientific fact that the Universe is entirely physical, and every single phenomenon in the Universe is a product of some physical process.

As the human mind / consciousness is a part of the Universe, we should consider it as a scientific fact that the human mind / consciousness is also a product of a physical process.
The human mind is produced by the brain in the same sense as the human urine is produced by kidneys. Nothing but a mundane biochemical process.

By the way, our entirely physical machines called computers directly demonstrate that many traditional attributes of the human mind are results of physical processes. This includes, among other things:
* intelligence
* creativity
* goals
* attention
* curiosity
* language
* self-reflection

There are not many intelligent tasks left in which humans are still better than AIs.

In a few decades we’ll have the first superhuman artificial general intelligence and/or mind uploading, and it will be the final nail in the coffin of the idea of soul.

Surprisingly many transhumanists have a distorted view of the future:
they think that the future society will be almost identical to our current society, but with cool implants and stuff.
They don't understand that the technology-enabled changes in politics and economics will be even bigger than the changes in technology.

A few examples of the existing trends and tech which will dramatically change our society:
1. Weapons
It's already possible to download and print a working firearm. You also can anonymously buy any weapon you want using distributed markets like Openbazaar and untraceable digital money like Dash. The Government can't switch them off without switching off the Internet.
2. Countries
You can legally buy a citizenship of a country. For example, the citizenship of a Caribbean nation of St. Kitts and Nevis is only $250 000, with no requirement to live in or even visit the country. Citizenship is a commodity now.
3. Corporations
In terms of economical and political power, big tech corporations like Facebook are more powerful than most countries. And they are already using their power to influence international politics (for example, Arab Spring). Some corporations (like Gazprom) are also owning private armies.

4. Energy
Tens of thousands of middle-class households in the US alone are living completely off the grid using wind and solar power. It's also already possible to semi-automatically grow your own food using hydroponics. More households will become economically autonomous in the coming years.

<mySummaryModeOn>



CryoNet is an interesting example of a long-living cryonics community / organization.
Judging by CryoNet Archives, its first iteration was created in 1988. A whole generation ago!
As far as I know, at least a dozen of various cryonics organizations have existed, and most of them died long before their 30th birthday.
Yet CryoNet continues to exist, in spite of facing many of the same real-world challenges.
Analyzing the reasons behind the CryoNet longevity could provide some useful insights for CSOs.
I can think of the following reasons:
- Deep automation of the core processes. Thanks to it, CryoNet can be run by a single person.
- Extreme frugality. It’s almost free to run it.
- Free membership. Thanks to the extreme frugality, no monetary contribution from the members is required for the long-term survival of the organization.
- Mobility. In the case of a crisis, CryoNet can be easily transferred to another (virtual) location
- The digital nature of the organization. It helps a lot that CryoNet doesn’t need to work with wetware, and all the associated problems – funeral directors, zoning laws, evil relatives etc etc.

I find the topic of highly durable data storage highly interesting. It’s also directly relevant to cryonics, as the digital data about the patient’s mind could play the key role in the future reanimation.
As far as I know, there are currently 4 types of highly durable data storage available to end-users:
Linear Tape-Open:
Claimed longevity: 30 years, Cost per TB: ~$50. Minimal price to buy the hardware etc: ~$300.
M-DISC:
1000 years. ~$130 / TB,  Min: ~$100
Nanofiche: 
1000 years, ~$10 000 / TB (?), min: $50 000.
Paper books on demand:
1000 years under some conditions,  > ~$1 000 000 / TB, min: $50
I know a German cryonicist who is also experimenting with granite as a data storage medium. A massive slab of granite could easily outlive all of the aforementioned types of media. But, to achieve a reasonable storage capacity, one need to find a way to utilize the entire volume of the stone, not only its surface.

In general, as IT people say, a data stored in only one place is a lost data.
Regardless of how good is the storage medium, you better have a lot of copies of the data, in geographically distributed locations.

For an extremely long preservation of cryopatients' data (including mind files), the following approach could be the best:
0. There is an international network of cryonicists
1. The participants upload their data to a cloud storage, and download the data of other participants
2. Additional copies of the data are stored on longlasting media (e.g Nanofiche, m-disks etc)
I would say that The InterPlanetary File System is definitely worth trying for it.
The cool name alone is a reason enough:)
Can you setup a node?
Judging by their documentation, IPFS requires a level of technical understanding far above the heads of the majority of cryonicists.
If we implement it in its current state of development, we'll spent too much time on the tech support and maintenance of our network.
I'll check IPFS in a few years, maybe they'll release a user-friendly client.


It would be naive to think that pCloud, Dropbox or any other centralized data storage service will last for centuries.
10 years is a more realistic life expectancy estimate.
To preserve the cryopatients' data, we should not rely on any single data storage service.
The storage must be as heterogenous and geographically distributed as possible.
A single DVD or a single Dropbox account will not preserve the data for centuries. But a dozen of differrent storage media types in a dozen of locations around the world could do it.

<mySummaryModeOn>


At some point in the future, it could become possible to cooldown an adult human to <77 K, and then revive them.
When will it happen? Why bother predicting the long-term future of cryonics?
Because the most rational course of action depends on it.
For example, let’s assume the reversible cryopreservation will be invented no earlier than 2500.
It’s unlikely that any of today’s CSOs will survive for so long. It means, in this scenario, most of the today’s patients will succumb to permadeath.
If this prediction is realistic, then we must assign a higher priority to preserving the patient’s mind data.
Unlike the fragile vessel of the human brain, data could be practically indestructible, as it can be copied ad infinitum.
Another rational course of action is to focus on a passive storage of patients that doesn’t require any human maintenance. It means, we should focus on chemical preservation at the room temperature, and on the storage in naturally cold regions (for example, in the so called “craters of eternal darkness” on the Moon).
On the other hand, if we assume that the reversible cryopreservation will be invented before 2030, we’ll have quite different priorities. In this scenario, supporting the cryobiology research by [G.F.] & Co should have a higher priority.
And chemical preservation and lunar storage should be “someday maybe” projects.
If the best prediction is between 2030 and 2100, then a rational cryonicist would have yet another set of priorities. For example, in this case, the fast-straight-freeze approach could make more sense, and more resources should be invested in passive storage projects.
What is your best prediction for the year of the first reversible cryopreservation of an adult human?
The Amara's law is a reasonable thumb rule. Most speculations about the future of technology are bullshit. Putting together a few trends is not enough to produce a good forecast.
The idea I want to convey is as follows: a good forecast of the future of cryonics could help us make more rational decisions on what to do today.
For example, if we expect that the first reanimation is centuries away, then a neuro-only chemical preservation is the most rational choice.
But if we expect that it’s only a few decades from now, a full-body cryo could be more reasonable.
To produce a good forecast of the future of some tech, an unique set of skills is necessary: the forecaster should possess the deep understanding of the field in question, AND understand how long-term tech forecasts should be made.
As far as I know, in the field of cryonics there are at least 3 people who fully meet the aforementioned criteria: [M.M.], [D.M.], [V.P.]. The list is by no means complete.
It would be highly interesting to hear their forecasts for the year of the first reversible cryopreservation of an adult human.


 
<mySummaryModeOff>


<mySummaryModeOn>



A good forecast should be based on as few assumptions as possible. And the assumptions should be explicitly described.
As an example, let’s consider the following assumptions:
1) Most of the CSOs that exist today will not exist in 2100.
It’s a reasonable assumption based on the observation that most organizations have a life expectancy lower than 50 years.
2) If a CSO ceases to exit, then its cryopatients will likely succumb to permadeath.
3) Ceteris paribus, the more of the patient’s body and brain we preserve, the higher is the chance of a successful reanimation.
Ideally, it would be nice to preserve everything, including the autonomic nervous system, gut flora etc.
4) There is no such minimal amount of information about the patient that is not worth preservation.
If we can’t preserve the whole body, we should at least preserve the brain.
If we can’t preserve the entire biochemistry of the brain, we should at least preserve its connectome. And so forth, down to the patient’s DNA, or even to the patient’s name.
Using these assumptions, let’s consider 2 scenarios.
Scenario A. We expect that we’ll need to store patients for centuries.
The assumptions #1 and #2 tell us that storing patients as they’re stored today is a bad idea.
In this scenario, the CSOs will cease to exist, and the cryopatients will succumb to permadeath.
Therefore, we must find a way to make the survival of patients more independent from the survival of CSOs.
A way to do it, is to follow the assumption #4, and use a chemical preservation at the room temperature. Of course, there are other possible solutions too.
Scenario B. We expect that the first reanimation will happen in the near future, and we’ll need to store patients only for a few decades at most.
In this scenario, we don’t worry so much about the #1 and #2. Instead, we follow the #3, and try to preserve as much of the patient as possible. The full body option is the way to go.
The entire thing is just an illustrative example, as it’s obviously incomplete.
But it would be interesting to analyze our assumptions about cryonics in a similar (but more complete) way.
I suspect that such analysis will show a severe discrepancy between the praxis and the assumptions of most CSOs.

The human brain a) is constantly renewing itself, b) is capable of sustaining huge damage, and c) can adapt to a loss of its part.
It allows as to make the transfer without any “original/copy” issues:
1. Create a realistic enough model of biological neural networks
2. Implement the model on hardware
3. Gradually - one neuron at a time - replace some neurons of the brain with our hardware
4. Wait till the brain adapts to its new part (=transfers some of its operations to the new part)
5. Repeat till the whole brain is replaced with our hardware
The result: true mind uploading. No copies produced. No originals destroyed. Just your mind/soul/consciousness/qualia/everything-you-need is transferred from the carbon computer to the silicon one.
In the same way as it was transferred from your 20yo brain to the current one - by gradually replacing its parts.﻿

# Written no later than 200203:


This is how one can prove the existence of some entity X:
1. Define X, describing its properties
2. Derive predictions from the properties of X
3. Check the predictions
4. If they are false, go to #7
5. Are there any good alternative explanations for the results of the checks? If false, go to #8
6. Describe the properties of X in more detail and go to #2
7. X does not exists. The end.
8. X exists. The end.




It's false that AIs can only follow a set pattern. There are many types of AI that can teach themselves how to play. Like AlphaGo

It's also false that it's impossibe for computers to experience human emotions.
There is a bullet-proof way to check it:
   1. Create a realistic simulation of a human neuron and implement it on hardware.
2. Gradually - one neuron a time - replace neurons in a human brain with our hardware. It will work because the components of the human brain are replaced all the time naturally, and the brain can sustain massive damage.
   3. You have a computer with human emotions. If humans have true emotions, then such computer will have them too.


As for gaming AIs, we can be sure about one thing. If alphaGo wins, then in a 10 years a software running on a mobile phone will beat every single professional Go player, even the best one in the world. Every single time.
The chess story will repeat itself.


Will the progess in AI result in Technological singularity?
It depends on how strong are limiting factors. If they are not strong enough to stop the exponential growth, the growth will continue.
Another variable is _when_ the growth stops. Maybe it stops on the near current level. Maybe it stops on the level of super-human intelligence orders of magnitude more powerful than the combined intelligence of all humans on the Earth. Or maybe even farther.
The theoretical existence of limiting factors does not necessary prohibits the technological singularity scenario. Technological singularity != unlimited growth.

Some people assume that one needs a vast amount of computing power to run a super-human intelligence. For an intelligence based on an ANN, it’s true, but there’re other ways too.
It’s possible that your smartphone could run an AI who will be significantly smarter than the average human by any measure. An AIXI-based AI or sonething.


Some people argue that it's impossible to reproduce the brain's function in a computer. See, for example, the Penrose's Gödelian Argument. But ther are several problems with it:
1) The argument is full of logical and mathematical errors.
2) It's possible to make a program capable of doing exactly that the human brain is doing when it generates the "solution" to the Gödelian kind of problems. So, it's false to assume that the brain is more powerful in this sense.



I think the main assumption of the idea of super-human AIs is that human is not the smartest possible creature in the universe, and probably very far from the smartest.
There is no reason to believe that the primitive naked ape created by biological evolution has achieved the maximum possible intelligence in the universe (especially if you consider the fact that the human brain is a terrible mess of evolutionary mistakes and bad engineering solutions). So, it’s a reasonable assumption.
If this assumption is correct, then it’s indeed possible to create an intelligence who will be far beyond humans.

Interestingly, this assumption is blind to our (in)ability to sustain exponential growth of computing power. It doesn’t say anything about the speed of the process of intelligence explosion nor about its mechanism.

Some people argue that economically viable applications where AGI are few. They're wrong. Artificial general intelligence is the intelligence of a machine that could successfully perform any intellectual task that a human being can (by definition). With a huge advantage over humans: it's possible to copy and scale it. Therefore, every single application where a human is must today, is economically viable for AGI.
And a sufficiently advanced AGI can do anything that a human can do, but better. This includes high-level “chef” tasks.
It's a multi-trillion-size market. The mother of all markets.


Some people say that robots cannot think creatively. But, taking into account the speed of progress in AI, humans will lose this advantage in no more than 20 years.


Do neural networks really have a limit on memory capacity?
I mean, if you teach a neural network on the base of 1 billion pictures instead of 1 million pictures, nothing will be "filled" in any sense. The neural network will just adjust its weights a bit more.

Don't believe people who say AGI will never be build. “Never” is a very strong word, especially in tech. More often than not “never” is later replaced with “yesterday”. A notorious example: Heavier-Than-Air_Flight_Is_Impossible
There is no reason to believe that AGI is impossible.


If an artificial heart can do the same job as a biological human heart, and is indistinguishable from the biological one for all practical purposes, then it’s correct to call it “real human heart”.
The same thing with the human intelligence. If an artificial intelligence is practically indistinguishable from the human one, than it’s the same thing.

There is also a way to transfer the human intelligence to silicon without any “simulation” issues:
The human brain a) is constantly renewing itself, b) is capable of sustaining huge damage, and c) can adapt to a loss of its part.
It allows us to transfer the intelligence using the following procedure:
   1. Create a realistic enough model of biological neural networks
2. Implement the model on hardware
3. Gradually - one neuron at a time - replace some neurons of the brain with our hardware
4. Wait till the brain adapts to its new part (=transfers some of its operations to the new part)
   5. Repeat till the whole brain is replaced with our hardware

I'm a biologist, and I can tell you from my professional perspective: death is not inevitable. There are organisms which don't age, and there are technological ways to approach the true immortality.
This kind of attitude is the reason why humanity defeated smallpox and many other diseases which were death sentences only a couple of decades ego. We did it with smallpox, we'll do it with aging. See Eradication_of_smallpox, Biological_immortality

I would define "living forever" as follows: living as long as it's theoretically possible to exist in our universe (or longer)

Give a creative person a million years, and he'll enjoy it. Give him a trillion years, and he'll enjoy it too. Give him a 10¹⁰⁰ years, and he'll still enjoy it. Because if you're creative, you'll always find a way to enjoy your life.
Some people simply can't be bored. There is so much to dream, think and do in our universe:)

Immortality can be solved by using a durable non-biological body + the ability to create digital backups of your mind (mind uploading)


I personally think that true immortality is only achievable in silico. Because one need a lot of backup copies and a hugely distributed body to outlive all the possible catastrophes and accidents. So, the most practical way to immortality is Mind_uploading. We are still far away from it, but it's achievable.


The typical arguments against immortality research expressed in the following metaphor.
At this very moment, a great library is burning. If we don't intervene, it will be burned down. Fire is a natural thing, of course. There is nothing unnatural about fire burning dead trees. It has happened for millions of years. Should we try to save the library? Or should we let the nature take its course? The answer is not as obvious as you think. Firstly, if we protect libraries from burning, at some point in the future there will be so many of them on the Earth, that the entire planet will be covered with libraries. Secondly, maybe it's a good idea to burn all libraries down to allow new and better libraries to replace them. Thirdly, the people who protect libraries are playing God! Who gave them the right to do the God's work of protecting stuff? Fourthly, imagine a library full of evil books about totalitarianism and tyranny. Isn't it a good thing that all libraries are eventually burned down, thus protecting us from such evil books? It's also important to note that librarians will get too bored if their beloved library would exist too long. You know, nothing is more boring than reading. And, most importantly, a fire-resistant library will lose its librarian identity, as we should not call such abomination a "library". It's obvious that fire gives all libraries their meaning of existence.
Not very convincing, isn't it?

One approach is to compare the brains of similarly related species.

If we assume that their brains have changed at the same pace, the difference between the brains in each pair could be roughly of the same magnitude.
There are dramatic differences, but the basic structure remains the same.
It’s of course a highly speculative approach, but it could provide some interesting clues.

How do I calculate the risk of my death on a 100 km trip using transport statistics?

Say, 300 deaths per 100 passenger-km. So, how high is the probability that I die on a 100km trip?

How should the probability of dying be calculated?
I’m trying to find a formula with these properties:
inputs: 1) number of deaths per billion passenger-kilometers, 2) length of the trip.
output: probability of me dying on this trip.
Preferably an exact formula, not an approximate.

It’s a bit more complicated than ratio. A probability should under no circumstances exceed 1, but if you use the ratio, it could be higher than 1 with some inputs. So, the approach is incorrect.

Imagine that there are 2 bln deaths per 1 bln passenger-hours. Riding on a nuke or something.
If you use a ratio, the probability will be higher than 1, which is wrong.
If X is the number of deaths per 1 bln passenger-hours, the probability of me dying in a 100 km trip should not exceed 1 regardless of the X. Even if X is astronomically big.
The correct formula for this exercise will never output a P>1 probability.
X can be higher than 1 bln. Because it’s passenger-hours, not just passengers.

It’s not a universal approach. It doesn’t work for all possible transport statistics. Replace 3 with 3 bln, for example.
I know that the result is not applicable in the real life. It’s about math, not about real risk management


I did a bit of research and derived this formula (not sure if it’s correct):
P = 1-(((10^9)-G)/(10^9))^L
G - number of deaths per billion passenger-kilometers
L - length of the trip, km
Using Wolfram Alpha I calculated that with G=3 and L=100 the P = 3x10^-7

The derivation is simple:
   1. replace “deaths” with black balls, “no-deaths” with white balls, and the “length of the trip” with taking balls L times from the urn which contains a certain mix (G) of black and white balls.
   2. calculate the probability of taking at least one black ball.

By the way, with G =3 and L=100, the value of P is ~ (299 999 955 450 / 1 000 000 000) * 10^-7.
The value (3x10^-7) is an approximation.

I can do combinatorics and some probability theory. Although I’m certainly not a math expert, and I was not sure if my solution was correct.
Unfortunately, Wolfram Alpha can’t solve such problems expressed in a plain language (at least, yet).

Other people prefer Android and PC and Linux exactly because they are infinitely customizable. And there are hundreds of millions of us.
3% of all desktop machines are running Linux (it’s a bit more popular than OS X worldwide).
It’s the matter of values.


The same arguments are applicable to desktop PCs.
Greater bulk, less software stability (because of non-static configuration) and (presumably) worse individual components.
Nevertheless, desktop PCs are extremely popular (OS X desktops are only 3% of the world market), because those few downsides are compensated by:
    * ability to replace and upgrade individual components (like video card or hard drive)
* ability to fully customize the hardware configuration
    * absence of vendor lock-ins (= more competition = better quality of individual components and better price)
I can build the best PC for my specific needs, be it gaming or programming or office work. I can’t do it with Mac.
It would be nice to have similarly customizable smartphones, even if they would be a bit more bulkier and more fragile.

Random changes, inheritance of those changes, and selection of the organisms with the best changes can produce very complex organisms from simple parts.
The biological evolution doesn’t work by “shaking the letters of a comedy novel for several billion years”. If we use the same metaphor, it works by introducing very small changes in the text and then by selecting the funniest novel among them. Like this:
“Mostlgia asn't at id usd to de...” (coefficient of fun: 7%)
“Mostlgia isn't at it usd to de...” (coefficient of fun: 10%)
“Nostlgia isn't what it usd to de...” (coefficient of fun: 14%)
“Nostalgia isn't what it usd to be...” (coefficient of fun: 16%)
“Nostalgia isn't what it used to be...” (coefficient of fun: 20%)

Imagine that you have made some very controversial discovery in the field of math. How would you proceed?
I would recommend to find a professional mathematician, who is open-minded and has a good reputation. Persuade him to actually proof-read your work. Pay him for his time if needed.
If he will find your work convincing, you will get a published paper (and fame) without any problems.
Another approach is to do it "humble".
If you try to publish your groundbreaking work in one piece, you'll inevitably get a lot of criticism. People are naturally skeptical if you present something groundbreaking.
A good solution is to cut your work in smaller pieces and publish them separately. Don't even mention Gödel in the first papers - you're just preparing the scientific community for your discovery. Describe some of your new ideas, some parts of your proof as separate pieces.
After you get these pieces published, you'll have a solid ground, and now your can do your final move: in the last paper you describe how those already accepted pieces are leading to your groundbreaking discovery.

Unfortunately(?), this is how science works: you must defend your ideas, even if the whole scientific community is against you.

There are millions of amateurs who think they are discovered something groundbreaking, but "mainstream scientists" are "too closed-minded" to appreciate their "discovery". I personally know a half-dozen of such amateurs.
The wast majority of them are just opinionated ignoramuses with an idée fixe. Crackpots. It's highly improbable what such amateur is right, and thousands of professionals are wrong.
And the scientific community is doing a good job to filter such people.

If you don't publish your ideas in peer reviewed journals, your ideas are just ideas, without any scientific value. And they'll end up in oblivion.
